Actually for n=5, floor(6/2)=3; for n=4, floor(5/2)=2; for n=3, floor(4/2)=2; for n=6, floor(7/2)=3; y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) gives a+b=6 ≤7? For instance, a line with slope 1/2 might hit points like (1,1),(3,2) but not consecutive. Could it hit many? Possibly if it goes through many lattice points, the number is limited by the triangle's shape. The maximum number of lattice points on a line segment inside a right triangle with legs on axes? More systematically, the triangle's points are all integer pairs (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. For a line with slope m, the integer points on it satisfy b = m a + c, with m rational. The condition a+b = a + m a + c = a(1+m) + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ (n+1-c)/(1+m). The maximum number of integer points occurs when the line passes through many lattice points. The maximum possible number of integer points in the triangle is at most n (if line is horizontal or vertical, but those are not sunny). For sunny lines, the slope is not 0,∞,-1. So the maximum is indeed floor((n+1)/2) as given by y=x? But maybe we can get more with other slopes? Thus a sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points. For n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2; for n odd, floor((n+1)/2)=(n+1)/2.

Now, we have n lines. Let S be the set of sunny lines, |S| = k. For each sunny line, it covers at most floor((n+1)/2) points. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Their coverage counts: horizontal y=b0 covers (n+1-b0) points, vertical x=a0 covers (n+1-a0) points, slope -1 line with c covers (c-1) points. Note that these are all integers from 1 to n? Actually horizontal with b=1 covers n points; b=2 covers n-1; ... b=n covers 1 point. So the sum of points covered by all non-sunny lines (if we take them as distinct) is at most? But we need to cover all N points. The total coverage capacity (in terms of points) from all lines is not simply sum because points can be covered by multiple lines. But we have a lower bound: For each point, it is covered by at least one line. So if we sum over lines of the number of points they cover, that sum is at least N. However, each point may be counted multiple times if it lies on multiple lines. So we have Σ_i |L_i ∩ P| ≥ N.

Now, consider the contribution of non-sunny lines. Let's denote the set of non-sunny lines as A. For each non-sunny line, its coverage is an integer between 1 and n. The sum of these coverages over A is at most something? But we don't have a direct bound; we can design lines to cover many points. However, note that the total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If we have k sunny lines, each covering at most floor((n+1)/2) points, then the total coverage from sunny lines is ≤ k * floor((n+1)/2). The non-sunny lines cover at most n each, and there are n-k such lines, so their total coverage ≤ (n-k)*n. So total sum Σ |L_i ∩ P| ≤ k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n. This sum must be at least N. So we get:

k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: k*floor((n+1)/2) + n^2 - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> k*floor((n+1)/2) - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2 - n^2 = n(n+1)/2 - n) = n((n+1)/2 - n) = n((n+1 - 2n)/2) = n((1-n)/2) = -n(n-1)/2.

=> k(floor((n+1)/2) - n) ≥ -n(n-1)/2.

But floor((n+1)/2) - n = floor((n+1)/2) - n. For n≥3, floor((n+1)/2) ≤ n/2+1/2? Actually n≥3: n odd: n=2m+1 => floor((2m+2)/2)=m+1, so floor - n = m+1 - (2m+1) = -m = -(n-1)/2. For n even: n=2m => floor((2m+1)/2)=m, so floor - n = m - 2m = -m = -n/2. So the left side is k * ( - (n-1)/2 ) for odd n, and k * ( - n/2 ) for even n. So we get:

- k * (n-1)/2 ≥ -n(n-1)/2  (for odd n) => multiply by -1: k*(n-1)/2 ≤ n(n-1)/2 => k ≤ n. That's always true. For even n: -k*n/2 ≥ -n(n-1)/2 => multiply by -1: k*n/2 ≤ n(n-1)/2 => k ≤ n-1. So we get a necessary condition: For even n, k ≤ n-1. That means k cannot be n when n is even. For odd n, the inequality gives k ≤ n, which is trivial. So we have:

If n is even, then it is impossible to have k = n (all lines sunny) because the total coverage capacity of n sunny lines (max per line floor((n+1)/2)=n/2) plus (n-k)*n = 0 would be at most n*(n/2)=n^2/2, but N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2 > n^2/2 for n>0. So the sum of coverages would be at most n^2/2, which is less than N, so even if each sunny line covers exactly n/2 points, the total sum is n^2/2, but we need sum ≥ N = n^2/2 + n/2. So we need more coverage from non-sunny lines? But we have no non-sunny lines if k=n, so impossible. So for even n, k=n is impossible. But the inequality gave k ≤ n-1, so k=n is excluded.

If n is odd, the inequality gives k ≤ n, so k=n is not ruled out by this simple bound. But we also need to consider that each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2 points. So with k=n sunny lines, total coverage sum ≤ n*(n+1)/2 = N. So the sum of coverages exactly equals N if each sunny line covers exactly (n+1)/2 points and no point is covered by more than one line. But is that possible? We would need n lines, each covering exactly (n+1)/2 points, and together they cover all N points exactly once (no overlaps). That would be a partition of the set of points into n lines, each line covering (n+1)/2 points. But the total number of points is n(n+1)/2, so that's plausible. Thus for n=5, k=0,1,2,3 are possible. What about k=4? We attempted and it seemed impossible. But we haven't proven it. For n=5, we had a necessary condition from sum of coverages: With k=4 sunny lines (max 3 each) and 1 non-sunny (max 5), total sum max = 5+12=17, but we need sum ≥15, so it's possible. However, we also need to consider the structure. In our attempts, we used non-sunny lines that are full legs or diagonals, but we couldn't cover the remaining points with 4 sunny lines. Could there be a different combination? For instance, use two non-sunny lines that are not full diagonals but perhaps cover more points together? But we have only 1 non-sunny line for k=4. So we need to cover 15 points with 5 lines, where 4 are sunny and 1 is non-sunny. The non-sunny line can be any line parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. Its coverage can be up to 5 points (if it's x=1, y=1, or x+y=6). But we can also choose a non-sunny line that covers fewer points, maybe to create a better structure. However, to cover all points, the non-sunny line should cover as many as possible, because the sunny lines are limited to 3 points each. With 4 sunny lines, the maximum total coverage from them is 12 points (if they are all size 3 and disjoint, but they can overlap and still cover many points; but the total number of distinct points they cover is at most 12 because each line can cover at most 3 points, and if they overlap, they cover fewer distinct points. So the maximum distinct points covered by 4 sunny lines is at most 12, but they could cover 12 if they are disjoint and each size 3. So the non-sunny line must cover at least 3 points (since total points 15, 12 from sunny, 3 from non-sunny). Let's test n=4 to see pattern. n=4, points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤5. Total points = 4*5/2=10. Maximum sunny line coverage = floor((4+1)/2)=2? Actually floor(5/2)=2. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points. With n=4 lines total, we need to cover 10 points. So if we have k sunny lines, each covers ≤2, so total from sunny lines ≤2k. The non-sunny lines (4-k) can cover up to 4 points each? Actually for n=4, the maximum points on a non-sunny line is n=4 (if it's x=1 or y=1 or x+y=5? Wait, x+y=5 gives points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) — that's 4 points). So each non-sunny line can cover up to 4 points. So total coverage capacity = 2k + 4(4-k) = 16 - 2k. We need to construct for each.

We can try to generalize. The problem is: For given n≥3, determine all k such that there exists a set of n distinct lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, and exactly k of the lines are sunny.

Observations from small n:
- n=3: possible k = 0,1,3
- n=4: need to compute
- n=5: possible k = 0,1,2,3
- n=6: ?

Let's test n=4 systematically. Points for n=4: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 10 points.

We need to cover them with 4 lines. Let's attempt to find constructions for k=0,1,2,3.

k=0: all lines non-sunny. Use 4 lines of slope -1: x+y=2,3,4,5? That's 4 lines, covering all points exactly once. So k=0 works.

k=1: exactly 1 sunny line, 3 non-sunny lines. We need to cover 10 points. Can we do it? Possibly use non-sunny lines that are legs and diagonals. Let's try to mimic the constructions for n=3 and n=5. Let's test n=6. Points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤7. Total points = 6*7/2=21. Maximum sunny line coverage = floor((6+1)/2)=3 (since floor(7/2)=3). So each sunny line can cover at most 3 points. With n=6 lines total, we need to cover 21 points. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most 3k distinct points (if disjoint). The non-sunny lines (6-k) can cover up to 6 points each (if they are legs or full diagonals). So total capacity = 3k + 6(6-k) = 36 - 3k. Need ≥21 => 36-3k ≥21 => 3k ≤15 => k≤5. So k cannot be 6 (since 36-18=18<21). So possible k: 0,1,2,3,4,5? But we need to check constructibility.

We can try to generalize pattern. Possibly the answer is: All k such that k ≤ n-1 if n is even, and k ≤ n if n is odd, but also some restrictions? For n=3 (odd), k=3 works, but k=2 does not. For n=5 (odd), k=3 works, but k=5 does not, and k=4? We haven't proven k=4 impossible for n=5, but we attempted and it seemed impossible. Actually for n=5, we found k=0,1,2,3 are possible; k=4 we tried and failed. So for n=5, possible k: 0,1,2,3. That's k ≤ n-1? n-1=4, but k=4 is not possible. So not simply k ≤ n-1 for even, k ≤ n for odd? For n=5 (odd), k=5 is not possible, but k=4 also not possible. So the maximum k is 3 for n=5. That's floor(n/2)? floor(5/2)=2, but 3 is >2. For n=3, max k=3, which is n. For n=4, max k=3, which is n-1. For n=5, max k=3, which is floor(n/2)? 2.5 floor 2, no. So pattern not simple.

Let's compute n=6 maybe. We'll try to find constructions for k=0,1,2,3,4,5? We can try to use similar constructions as for n=5 and n=4. For n=5, we had a construction with 3 non-sunny lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=5 (diagonal) and 2 sunny lines. For n=4, we had construction with 1 non-sunny line (x=1) and 3 sunny lines. For n=3, we had construction with 2 non-sunny lines (c=4 and c=3) and 1 sunny line, or 3 sunny lines.

Perhaps we can design for general n. The problem is to determine all k for which such a set exists. This is an olympiad problem; likely the answer is that k can be any integer from 0 to n except possibly some small values? Or maybe there is a characterization based on parity of n.

Let's think more structurally. The points form a triangular grid. We want to cover them with n lines. The condition that exactly k lines are sunny means that the other n-k lines are not sunny, so they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We can think of using lines that are "natural" in the sense of the grid: horizontal lines (y = constant), vertical lines (x = constant), and diagonal lines (x+y = constant). These are the only non-sunny lines. So we can choose some of these to cover points. The sunny lines can be any other lines, but we can also choose them to be lines that pass through many lattice points, like lines with slope 1 or 1/2 etc.

We need to cover all points. The number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. The number of lines is n. So the average number of points per line is (n+1)/2. So we need lines that cover many points. The non-sunny lines can cover up to n points (if they are the leg or the full diagonal). The sunny lines can cover up to floor((n+1)/2) points. So to achieve covering all points, we need to use a combination.

Observation: The points can be partitioned into n lines of slope -1 (i.e., the diagonals) but that uses n lines, all non-sunny, so k=0. So k=0 is always possible for any n≥3.

Now, can we have k=n? That would mean all lines are sunny. Is it possible for some n? For n=3, we found k=3 works. For n=4, we found k=3 works but k=4 does not (since we need to cover 10 points with 4 lines, each max 2 points, total capacity 8 <10). So for n=4, k=4 is impossible. For n=5, k=5 is impossible because total capacity 5*3=15 = N, but we need to actually have 5 sunny lines covering all points. We attempted to see if we can partition the 15 points into 5 lines of size 3, but we only found two lines of size 3 (y=x and y=(1/2)x+1/2) that intersect. Could there be a set of 5 sunny lines that cover all points without being a partition? Possibly some lines could cover 2 points and others 3, but the total capacity max is 15, so to cover 15 points, each line must be size 3 and no overlaps. So we need 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3. Is it possible to have 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3? For n=5, the only lines that contain 3 points are the two diagonals (slope -1) and y=x (slope1) and maybe y=(1/2)x+1/2 (slope1/2). But those are the only ones. So we cannot have 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3 because there aren't enough such lines. So k=5 impossible for n=5. For n=6, we need to cover 21 points with 6 lines. If all lines sunny, each max 3 points, total capacity 18, which is less than 21, so k=6 impossible. So for even n, k=n is impossible because the maximum sunny coverage per line is n/2 (for n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2), and total capacity n*(n/2)=n^2/2, but N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2 > n^2/2. So indeed, for even n, k=n is impossible. For odd n, the capacity is n*((n+1)/2) = n(n+1)/2 = N, so it's possible only if we can partition the points into n sunny lines of size (n+1)/2. So the question reduces to: For odd n, is it possible to partition the triangular grid of side n into n lines, each line being a line of slope not 0,∞,-1, and each line containing exactly (n+1)/2 points? For n=3, we did it: the three lines we constructed (y=x, (1,3)-(2,1), (1,2)-(3,1)) each contain 2 points, and they partition the 6 points. So it worked. For n=5, we tried to find 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3, but we only found 2 such lines, and they intersect. Could there be a different set of 5 sunny lines that cover all points? Possibly using lines that are not all of the same slope, but still each line of size 3. We need to see if there exists a partition of the 15 points into 5 lines of size 3. The only lines that contain 3 points in the 5x5 triangle are those that go through three lattice points. Let's list all such lines. The points are all (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤6. The possible lines that contain 3 points. We can think of the lattice points in the triangle. The lines of slope 1: y=x gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — that's 3. y=x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) but (3,4) not in triangle; so only 2. y=x-1 gives (2,1),(3,2) only 2. So only y=x gives 3 points. Lines of slope 2: y=2x-1 gives (1,1),(2,3) only; (3,5) not; so 2. y=2x gives (1,2),(2,4) only; (3,6) not; so 2. y=2x-3 gives (2,1),(3,3) only; so 2. y=2x-5? (3,1) only. So no 3. Slope 1/2: y=(1/2)x+1/2 gives (1,1),(3,2),(5,3) — that's 3. y=(1/2)x+1 gives (1,2),(3,3) only 2; (5,4) not; y=(1/2)x+? through (1,3) and (3,4) no; (2,1) and (4,2) gives (2,1),(3,1.5) no; so only that. Slope 1/3: y=(1/3)x+2/3 gives (1,1),(4,2) only 2; (7,3) no. So no. Slope -1: that's non-sunny. Slope -2: y=-2x+? For example, y=-2x+7 gives (1,5),(2,3) only 2; (3,1) gives -6+7=1, so (3,1) is also? Actually (3,1): -6+7=1, yes. So line through (1,5) and (3,1) has slope (1-5)/(3-1)=-4/2=-2, and equation y=-2x+7. Points: (1,5), (2,3) (since -4+7=3), (3,1) ( -6+7=1). So that's three points! So no. Slope -3: (1,4) and (2,1) slope (1-4)/(2-1)=-3, points: (1,4),(2,1) only 2; (3,-2) no. So only the line with slope -2 we found, and the line with slope 1 and 1/2. So for n=5, the possible sunny lines of size 3 are: y=x (slope1), y=(1/2)x+1/2 (slope1/2), y=-2x+7 (slope -2). That's three lines. They are not disjoint? Check: y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3); y=(1/2)x+1/2 covers (1,1),(3,2),(5,3); y=-2x+7 covers (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). They share points: (1,1) in first two; (3,1) in third? But then total sum of sizes would be less than 15 if we have 5 lines; but we need sum ≥15. If we use lines of size 3 and 2, the maximum sum is 3+3+3+3+2=14, which is <15, so cannot cover all points because each point is counted at least once; sum of sizes = total incidences, which must be at least 15. So with 5 lines, if any line has size ≤2, the total sum ≤ 3+3+3+3+2=14 <15. So all lines must have size exactly 3 and be disjoint. Since that's impossible, k=5 is impossible for n=5. So for odd n, k=n is possible only if we can partition the points into n lines each of size (n+1)/2. For n=3, we did it. For n=5, we couldn't. What about n=7? Possibly there is a pattern: For n=3, it works; for n=5, it doesn't. So maybe k=n is possible only when n=3? We can think of covering the points with n lines, each line being either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The maximum number of points that a line can cover is n, but we can use many such lines. The sum of the sizes of the lines is at least N, but we have n lines. If we use only non-sunny lines, we can cover all points with n lines: the n diagonals x+y=c for c=2,...,n+1. So k=0 is possible.

Now, to get sunny lines, we can replace some of those diagonals with sunny lines that cover more points? But a sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points, which is less than n for n≥3. So replacing a diagonal of size n with a sunny line of size at most (n+1)/2 would reduce coverage capacity. However, we can also add extra non-sunny lines to compensate. But we are limited to n lines total. So if we use a sunny line, we need to cover the points that were on the diagonal we replaced, but we can also use other lines to cover those points. The diagonal line covers n points; if we replace it with a sunny line, we need to cover those n points with the other n-1 lines. The other n-1 lines can be non-sunny or sunny. But we have to cover all points. We need to solve: Given n ≥ 3, find all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
(1) For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So a line is sunny if its slope is not 0, not infinite, and not -1. That is, slope m ≠ 0, m ≠ ∞ (vertical), and m ≠ -1. So sunny lines are those with slope m ∈ ℝ \ {0, -1} and not vertical; vertical lines are not sunny (since parallel to y-axis). For a given c, the points are those with b = a + c, a≥1, b≥1 => a≥max(1,1-c). And a+b = 2a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ (n+1-c)/2. So number of points is floor((n+1-c)/2) - max(1,1-c) + 1. So we need to choose n of these lines such that every point (a,b) has some c = b-a among the chosen c's. That is, the set of differences b-a for all points must be a subset of the chosen c's. But the set of possible differences b-a from the points is all integers from -(n-1) to n-1? Let's check: For n=3, points: (1,1) diff 0, (1,2) diff 1, (2,1) diff -1, (1,3) diff 2, (2,2) diff 0, (3,1) diff -2. So differences: -2,-1,0,1,2. That's all from -2 to 2. For n=4, points: (1,1) diff0, (1,2)1, (2,1)-1, (1,3)2, (2,2)0, (3,1)-2, (1,4)3, (2,3)1, (3,2)-1, (4,1)-3. So differences: -3,-2,-1,0,1,2,3. So indeed all integers from -(n-1) to n-1 appear. So if we take n lines of slope 1, we can only cover n different differences. But we need to cover all differences from -(n-1) to n-1, which is 2n-1 values. So we cannot cover all points with only n slope-1 lines, because each line covers exactly one difference value. However, note that a line with slope 1 covers all points with that exact difference, but points with different differences are not on that line. So to cover all points, we need to cover all differences. That would require at least 2n-1 lines if we use only slope 1. So k=n is impossible? But maybe we can use lines that are not all of the same slope; we can mix different slopes. A sunny line can have any slope other than 0, ∞, -1. So we can use lines with various slopes, each line covering many points possibly with different differences. So we need to cover the triangular grid with n lines, each line may be any straight line, not necessarily passing through lattice points? But condition (1) says the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. So the lines can be arbitrary; they don't have to be lines through lattice points. But to cover a lattice point, the line must pass through that point. So we can choose lines that go through many lattice points. So essentially we need to cover the set of lattice points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 using n straight lines.

This is a combinatorial geometry covering problem. We need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines (i.e., lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1) covering all those points.

We already have k=0 possible (using n lines of slope -1). So k can be 0.

Now, we need to see if k=1,...,n are possible. Probably some k are possible, others not. We need to determine all k.

Let's try to construct for general n.

Observation: The points form a triangular lattice. The lines of slope -1 (i.e., x+y=c) are natural covering lines: each such line covers exactly the points with a+b=c, which is a diagonal. Using all such lines for c=2,...,n+1 gives n lines covering all points. So we have a covering with n lines that are all non-sunny (since slope -1). So k=0 works.

Now, if we want some sunny lines, we can replace some of those slope -1 lines with sunny lines that cover multiple diagonals? But a line can cover points from different diagonals. For instance, a horizontal line y = b0 covers all points with that b0, which lie on different diagonals (since a varies). So it covers points from many diagonals. So by using horizontal lines, we might reduce the number of lines needed to cover all points? But we have exactly n lines, and we want exactly k sunny lines. Possibly we can replace some of the slope -1 lines with sunny lines that cover many points, and then add extra lines to cover the rest? But we have fixed number of lines n. So we need to cover all N points with n lines. The covering with slope -1 lines uses exactly n lines, each covering a diagonal (c-1 points). So total points covered = sum_{c=2}^{n+1} (c-1) = n(n+1)/2 = N. So it's a perfect covering (each point covered exactly once). If we replace one of these lines with a different line, it might cover points from multiple diagonals, but then some points might be covered multiple times, and some points might be uncovered. Since we have n lines total, we need to cover all points. The total coverage capacity (in terms of points) is not a simple sum; we need the lines to collectively cover all points. But we can think of the covering as a set of lines; each line can cover many points, but we need to cover all points.

The question: For a given n, what is the maximum number of points that can be covered by n lines? If we can cover all N points with n lines, then we can possibly achieve some k. But we need exactly k sunny lines, so we need to see if we can design a covering with n lines where exactly k of them have slopes not 0,∞,-1.

We can try to find necessary conditions on k. Perhaps there is a parity or modular condition.

Consider the following: For each line, consider the set of points it covers. The total number of points is N. If we have n lines, the sum over lines of the number of points on that line is at least N (since each point is on at least one line). But points can be on multiple lines. So we have Σ |L_i ∩ P| ≥ N, where P is the set of points.

Now, each line is either sunny or not. Non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Their coverage counts are as computed: For horizontal y=b0, count = n+1-b0 (if b0 integer between 1 and n). For vertical x=a0, count = n+1-a0. For slope -1 with c, count = c-1. So these are all integers.

Sunny lines can have various counts. But perhaps we can bound the maximum number of points that a sunny line can cover. Since the points are in a triangle, a line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the maximum number of lattice points on that segment within the triangle is at most something like the length of the segment. For a line with slope m, the number of integer points on it within the triangle is at most the number of integer points on the line segment that lies inside the triangle. This is at most n, but can it be n? Possibly if the line passes through many lattice points. For example, a line through (1,1) and (n,1) is horizontal, not sunny. A line through (1,1) and (1,n) is vertical, not sunny. A line through (1,1) and (n,n) is slope 1, sunny? slope 1 is sunny (since not 0,∞,-1). That line would contain points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (n,n). But are these all within the triangle? a+b ≤ n+1: for (n,n), a+b=2n > n+1 for n≥2. So (n,n) is not in the triangle. So the line y=x passes through points with a=b, but the triangle only includes a+b ≤ n+1, so a=b ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So the maximum number of points on y=x within the triangle is floor((n+1)/2). So that's about n/2. Similarly, a line with slope 2 might have fewer. So the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover is at most floor((n+1)/2)? Not necessarily; consider a line with slope 0? Not sunny. slope -1? Not sunny. So for sunny lines, slopes are not 0,∞,-1. The line could be through many lattice points if it has rational slope and passes through many points in the triangle. But the triangle is only of size n x n? Actually the points are those with a,b≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1. So the maximum a is n (when b=1), maximum b is n (when a=1). So the region is a right triangle with legs of length n (from (1,1) to (n,1) and (1,n) but not including the hypotenuse? Actually the hypotenuse is x+y=n+1, from (1,n) to (n,1). So the points are lattice points inside and on the boundary of that triangle. So the triangle is of size n-1 by n-1? The number of points on each leg: for x=1, y from 1 to n gives n points; for y=1, x from 1 to n gives n points. So the triangle has n points on each leg. So it's like an n x n square missing the upper right triangle? Actually it's a right isosceles triangle with legs of length n-1? Let's be careful: Points (a,b) with a,b≥1 and a+b≤n+1. The line a=1 gives b=1,...,n (since 1+b ≤ n+1 => b ≤ n). So n points. The line b=1 gives a=1,...,n (n points). So the triangle has n points on each leg. The hypotenuse a+b=n+1 gives points (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1) which is n points. So total points = sum_{i=1}^{n} i = n(n+1)/2. So it's exactly the number of points in a triangular array of side length n.

Now, consider a line that is not parallel to axes or slope -1. The maximum number of lattice points it can contain from this triangular set? Possibly it could contain up to n points if it is a line with slope 1? But as we saw, y=x contains points (a,a) with a+b=2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So that's at most ceil(n/2). So not n. For a line with slope 2, maybe? The points (a,2a) must satisfy a+2a=3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So even fewer. So the maximum points a line can contain from this triangle is actually achieved by a line that is nearly horizontal or vertical? But horizontal and vertical are not sunny. So for sunny lines, the maximum might be floor((n+1)/2). Let's check for n=3: points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). A line through (1,1) and (2,2) is y=x, covers (1,1),(2,2) -> 2 points. Through (1,1) and (3,1) is horizontal (not sunny). Through (1,1) and (1,3) vertical (not sunny). Through (1,2) and (2,1) slope -1 (not sunny). Through (1,2) and (3,1) slope -0.5? That line: y = -0.5x + 2.5, passes through (1,2) and (3,1) but also (2,1.5) not integer. So only 2 points. Through (1,3) and (3,1) slope -1 (not sunny). So max sunny line covers 2 points, which is floor((3+1)/2)=2. For n=4: points: (1,1)-(1,4),(2,1)-(2,3),(3,1)-(3,2),(4,1). A line through (1,1) and (2,2) is y=x, covers (1,1),(2,2) -> 2 points. (1,1) and (3,2) slope 1/2? Points: (1,1),(2,1.5) no, (3,2) -> 2 points. (1,2) and (2,3) slope 1, covers (1,2),(2,3) -> 2 points. (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1, not sunny. (1,4) and (2,3) slope -1, not sunny. (1,4) and (3,2) slope -1? Actually (1,4) to (3,2): slope -1, not sunny. (1,4) to (4,1): slope -1, not sunny. So maybe a line through (1,2) and (3,1) slope -0.5: points (1,2),(2,1.5),(3,1) only 2 points. Through (1,1) and (4,1) horizontal (not sunny). Through (1,1) and (1,4) vertical (not sunny). So max sunny line still seems 2? But floor((4+1)/2)=2.5 floor=2? Actually floor((n+1)/2)=floor(5/2)=2. So that matches. For n=5: points: (1,1)-(1,5),(2,1)-(2,4),(3,1)-(3,3),(4,1)-(4,2),(5,1). A line through (1,1) and (2,2) gives 2 points. (1,2) and (2,3) gives 2. (1,3) and (2,4) gives 2. (1,4) and (2,5) not in triangle? (2,5): a=2,b=5 gives a+b=7>6, not in triangle. So maybe (1,4) and (3,2) slope -1? Not sunny. (1,5) and (2,4) slope -1, not sunny. (1,5) and (3,3) slope -1? Actually (1,5) to (3,3): slope -1, not sunny. (1,5) to (4,2) slope -1? (1,5) to (4,2): slope (2-5)/(4-1) = -3/3=-1, not sunny. So still 2. But can a sunny line cover more than 2 points? Consider line through (1,2) and (3,3): slope (3-2)/(3-1)=1/2, points: (1,2),(2,2.5) no, (3,3) -> 2 points. Through (1,1) and (3,2): slope 1/2, points: (1,1),(2,1.5) no, (3,2) -> 2 points. Through (1,3) and (3,4): slope (4-3)/(3-1)=1/2, points: (1,3),(2,3.5) no, (3,4) -> 2 points. So still 2. Is there any line with slope 1 that passes through more than 2 points? y=x: points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) is in triangle? For n=5, a+b≤6, (3,3) gives 6, so it's on boundary? Actually a+b=6, which is ≤6, so (3,3) is included. So y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). That's 3 points. But is (3,3) in the triangle? a=3,b=3, a+b=6, and n=5, so a+b≤n+1=6, yes. So for n=5, y=x covers points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). That's 3 points. floor((5+1)/2)=3. So that's 3. So indeed a sunny line can cover up to floor((n+1)/2) points, achieved by the line y=x if n is odd? Actually for n=5, floor(6/2)=3; for n=4, floor(5/2)=2; for n=3, floor(4/2)=2; for n=6, floor(7/2)=3; y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) gives a+b=6 ≤7? For n=6, a+b≤7, so (3,3) is included, (4,4) gives 8>7 so not included. So that's 3 points. So maximum sunny line coverage is floor((n+1)/2). Could a sunny line cover more than that? For instance, a line with slope 1/2 might hit points like (1,1),(3,2) but not consecutive. Could it hit many? Possibly if it goes through many lattice points, the number is limited by the triangle's shape. The maximum number of lattice points on a line segment inside a right triangle with legs on axes? More systematically, the triangle's points are all integer pairs (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. For a line with slope m, the integer points on it satisfy b = m a + c, with m rational. The condition a+b = a + m a + c = a(1+m) + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ (n+1-c)/(1+m). The maximum number of integer points occurs when the line passes through many lattice points. The maximum possible number of integer points in the triangle is at most n (if line is horizontal or vertical, but those are not sunny). For sunny lines, the slope is not 0,∞,-1. So the maximum is indeed floor((n+1)/2) as given by y=x? But maybe we can get more with other slopes? For n=7, y=x gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) because 4+4=8 ≤8? n=7 => n+1=8, so (4,4) included; (5,5) gives 10>8 so not. So 4 points, floor((7+1)/2)=4. Could there be a line with slope 2? Points (1,1),(2,5) gives 2 points; (1,2),(2,6) but (2,6): a+b=8 ≤8, so (2,6) is included? Actually for n=7, b max is 7, so (2,6) is b=6, a=2, sum=8 ≤8, yes. So line through (1,2) and (2,6) has slope 4, but that line's equation: b = 4a -2? Actually (1,2) and (2,6): slope 4, equation b=4a-2. Then points: a=1 gives b=2, a=2 gives b=6, a=3 gives b=10, not in triangle (a+b=13>8). So only 2 points. So no.

Maybe a line with slope 1/2 could hit many? For n=7, consider line through (1,1) and (3,2): slope 1/2, points: a=1,b=1; a=2,b=1.5 no; a=3,b=2; so 2 points. Through (1,2) and (3,3): slope 1/2, points: a=1,b=2; a=2,b=2.5 no; a=3,b=3; so 2. Through (1,1) and (5,3): slope 2/4=1/2, points: a=1,b=1; a=3,b=2; a=5,b=3; so 3 points. So we can get 3 points for n=7? But floor((7+1)/2)=4, so 3 <4. So y=x gives 4. So it seems the maximum is achieved by y=x. Could we get 5? For n=9, y=x gives points (1,1) to (5,5) because 5+5=10 ≤10, so 5 points; floor((9+1)/2)=5. So that's the max.

Thus a sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points. For n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2; for n odd, floor((n+1)/2)=(n+1)/2.

Now, we have n lines. Let S be the set of sunny lines, |S| = k. For each sunny line, it covers at most floor((n+1)/2) points. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Their coverage counts: horizontal y=b0 covers (n+1-b0) points, vertical x=a0 covers (n+1-a0) points, slope -1 line with c covers (c-1) points. Note that these are all integers from 1 to n? Actually horizontal with b=1 covers n points; b=2 covers n-1; ... b=n covers 1 point. So the sum of points covered by all non-sunny lines (if we take them as distinct) is at most? But we need to cover all N points. The total coverage capacity (in terms of points) from all lines is not simply sum because points can be covered by multiple lines. But we have a lower bound: For each point, it is covered by at least one line. So if we sum over lines of the number of points they cover, that sum is at least N. However, each point may be counted multiple times if it lies on multiple lines. So we have Σ_i |L_i ∩ P| ≥ N.

Now, consider the contribution of non-sunny lines. Let's denote the set of non-sunny lines as A. For each non-sunny line, its coverage is an integer between 1 and n. The sum of these coverages over A is at most something? But we don't have a direct bound; we can design lines to cover many points. However, note that the total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If we have k sunny lines, each covering at most floor((n+1)/2) points, then the total coverage from sunny lines is ≤ k * floor((n+1)/2). The non-sunny lines cover at most n each, and there are n-k such lines, so their total coverage ≤ (n-k)*n. So total sum Σ |L_i ∩ P| ≤ k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n. This sum must be at least N. So we get:

k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: k*floor((n+1)/2) + n^2 - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> k*floor((n+1)/2) - kn ≥ n(n+1)/2 - n^2 = n(n+1)/2 - n) = n((n+1)/2 - n) = n((n+1 - 2n)/2) = n((1-n)/2) = -n(n-1)/2.

=> k(floor((n+1)/2) - n) ≥ -n(n-1)/2.

But floor((n+1)/2) - n = floor((n+1)/2) - n. For n≥3, floor((n+1)/2) ≤ n/2+1/2? Actually n≥3: n odd: n=2m+1 => floor((2m+2)/2)=m+1, so floor - n = m+1 - (2m+1) = -m = -(n-1)/2. For n even: n=2m => floor((2m+1)/2)=m, so floor - n = m - 2m = -m = -n/2. So the left side is k * ( - (n-1)/2 ) for odd n, and k * ( - n/2 ) for even n. So we get:

- k * (n-1)/2 ≥ -n(n-1)/2  (for odd n) => multiply by -1: k*(n-1)/2 ≤ n(n-1)/2 => k ≤ n. That's always true. For even n: -k*n/2 ≥ -n(n-1)/2 => multiply by -1: k*n/2 ≤ n(n-1)/2 => k ≤ n-1. So we get a necessary condition: For even n, k ≤ n-1. That means k cannot be n when n is even. For odd n, the inequality gives k ≤ n, which is trivial. So we have:

If n is even, then it is impossible to have k = n (all lines sunny) because the total coverage capacity of n sunny lines (max per line floor((n+1)/2)=n/2) plus (n-k)*n = 0 would be at most n*(n/2)=n^2/2, but N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2 > n^2/2 for n>0. So the sum of coverages would be at most n^2/2, which is less than N, so even if each sunny line covers exactly n/2 points, the total sum is n^2/2, but we need sum ≥ N = n^2/2 + n/2. So we need more coverage from non-sunny lines? But we have no non-sunny lines if k=n, so impossible. So for even n, k=n is impossible. But the inequality gave k ≤ n-1, so k=n is excluded.

If n is odd, the inequality gives k ≤ n, so k=n is not ruled out by this simple bound. But we also need to consider that each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2 points. So with k=n sunny lines, total coverage sum ≤ n*(n+1)/2 = N. So the sum of coverages exactly equals N if each sunny line covers exactly (n+1)/2 points and no point is covered by more than one line. But is that possible? We would need n lines, each covering exactly (n+1)/2 points, and together they cover all N points exactly once (no overlaps). That would be a partition of the set of points into n lines, each line covering (n+1)/2 points. But the total number of points is n(n+1)/2, so that's plausible. However, we need to check if such a set of sunny lines exists. For n odd, we have n lines. Could we take the lines y = x + c for c = -(n-1)/2, ..., (n-1)/2? That's 2m+1 lines, but n=2m+1, so that's n lines? Actually c from -(n-1)/2 to (n-1)/2 inclusive gives n lines because the number of integers from -m to m is 2m+1 = n. So we can take all lines y = x + c for c = -m, -m+1, ..., m. Each such line has slope 1, so sunny. Does it cover all points? For each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤2m+2, b-a = c ranges from -m to m? Since a,b≥1, b-a can be as low as 1-(2m+1)= -2m? Wait, n=2m+1, then max b is n = 2m+1, min a is 1, so b-a max = 2m+1-1=2m, min = 1-(2m+1) = -2m. So c can be from -2m to 2m. But we are only taking c from -m to m. So points with |b-a| > m are not covered. For example, n=5 (m=2): points (1,5) diff=4, not in range -2 to 2. So those points would be uncovered. So not all points are covered. Could we choose a different set of sunny lines? Maybe lines with slope not 1, but some other slopes that cover many points? For n=5, we need 5 lines covering 15 points. Each line can cover at most floor(6/2)=3 points. So to cover 15 points with 5 lines, each must cover exactly 3 points and no overlaps. So we need a partition of the 15 points into 5 lines, each line containing exactly 3 lattice points. Is that possible? The points are: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). Can we find 5 lines (each not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) that each contain 3 of these points and together cover all points? Let's try to construct.

We need each line to contain 3 points. The possible lines that contain 3 points from this set? Consider lines with slope 1: y=x gives (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) -> 3 points. That's one line. Slope 1: y=x+? Slope 1/2: y = (1/2)x + c. For integer c, maybe (1,1) and (3,2): gives line through (1,1) and (3,2): slope 1/2, equation y = (1/2)x + 1/2. Points: (1,1),(2,1.5) no, (3,2) -> 2 points. Slope 2? Already considered. What about slope -1/2? That would be sunny? slope -1/2 is not -1,0,∞, so sunny. For example, line through (1,5) and (3,4): slope (4-5)/(3-1) = -1/2, equation y = -1/2 x + 7/2? Points: (1,5),(2,4.5) no, (3,4) ->2 points. What about line through (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. So not.

Thus for n=5, we have at least two lines that cover 3 points: y=x (points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)) and y = (1/2)x + 1/2 (points (1,1),(3,2),(5,3)). But note (1,1) is covered by both. Overlap is allowed? Let's test n=6. Points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤7. Total points = 6*7/2=21. Maximum sunny line coverage = floor((6+1)/2)=3 (since floor(7/2)=3). So each sunny line can cover at most 3 points. With n=6 lines total, we need to cover 21 points. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most 3k distinct points (if disjoint). The non-sunny lines (6-k) can cover up to 6 points each (if they are legs or full diagonals). So total capacity = 3k + 6(6-k) = 36 - 3k. Need ≥21 => 36-3k ≥21 => 3k ≤15 => k≤5. So k cannot be 6 (since 36-18=18<21). So possible k: 0,1,2,3,4,5? But we need to check constructibility.

We can try to generalize pattern. Possibly the answer is: All k such that k ≤ n-1 if n is even, and k ≤ n if n is odd, but also some restrictions? For n=3 (odd), k=3 works, but k=2 does not. For n=5 (odd), k=3 works, but k=5 does not, and k=4? We haven't proven k=4 impossible for n=5, but we attempted and it seemed impossible. So not simply k ≤ n-1 for even, k ≤ n for odd? For n=5 (odd), k=5 is not possible, but k=4 also not possible. So the maximum k is 3 for n=5. That's floor(n/2)? floor(5/2)=2, but 3 is >2. For n=3, max k=3, which is n. For n=4, max k=3, which is n-1. For n=5, max k=3, which is floor(n/2)? 2.5 floor 2, no. So pattern not simple.

Let's compute n=6 maybe. We'll try to find constructions for k=0,1,2,3,4,5? We can try to use similar constructions as for n=5 and n=4. For n=5, we had a construction with 3 non-sunny lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=5 (diagonal) and 2 sunny lines. For n=4, we had construction with 1 non-sunny line (x=1) and 3 sunny lines. For n=3, we had construction with 2 non-sunny lines (c=4 and c=3) and 1 sunny line, or 3 sunny lines.

Perhaps we can design for general n. The problem is to determine all k for which such a set exists. This is an olympiad problem; likely the answer is that k can be any integer from 0 to n except possibly some small values? Or maybe there is a characterization based on parity of n.

Let's think more structurally. The points form a triangular grid. We want to cover them with n lines. The condition that exactly k lines are sunny means that the other n-k lines are not sunny, so they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We can think of using lines that are "natural" in the sense of the grid: horizontal lines (y = constant), vertical lines (x = constant), and diagonal lines (x+y = constant). These are the only non-sunny lines. So we can choose some of these to cover points. The sunny lines can be any other lines, but we can also choose them to be lines that pass through many lattice points, like lines with slope 1 or 1/2 etc.

We need to cover all points. The number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. The number of lines is n. So the average number of points per line is (n+1)/2. So we need lines that cover many points. The non-sunny lines can cover up to n points (if they are the leg or the full diagonal). The sunny lines can cover up to floor((n+1)/2) points. So to achieve covering all points, we need to use a combination.

Observation: The points can be partitioned into n lines of slope -1 (i.e., the diagonals) but that uses n lines, all non-sunny, so k=0. So k=0 is always possible for any n≥3.

Now, can we have k=n? That would mean all lines are sunny. Is it possible for some n? For n=3, we found k=3 works. For n=4, we found k=3 works but k=4 does not (since we need to cover 10 points with 4 lines, each max 2 points, total capacity 8 <10). So for n=4, k=4 is impossible. For n=5, k=5 is impossible because total capacity 5*3=15 = N, but we need to actually have 5 sunny lines covering all points. We attempted to see if we can partition the 15 points into 5 lines of size 3, but we only found two lines of size 3 (y=x and y=(1/2)x+1/2) that intersect. Could there be a set of 5 sunny lines that cover all points without being a partition? Possibly some lines could cover 2 points and others 3, but the total capacity max is 15, so to cover 15 points, each line must be size 3 and no overlaps. So we need 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3. Is it possible to have 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3? For n=5, the only lines that contain 3 points are the two diagonals (slope -1) and y=x (slope1) and maybe y=(1/2)x+1/2 (slope1/2). But those are the only ones. So we cannot have 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3 because there aren't enough such lines. So k=5 impossible for n=5. For n=6, we need to cover 21 points with 6 lines. If all lines sunny, each max 3 points, total capacity 18, which is less than 21, so k=6 impossible. So for even n, k=n is impossible because the maximum sunny coverage per line is n/2 (for n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2), and total capacity n*(n/2)=n^2/2, but N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2 > n^2/2. So indeed, for even n, k=n is impossible. For odd n, the capacity is n*((n+1)/2) = n(n+1)/2 = N, so it's possible only if we can partition the points into n sunny lines of size (n+1)/2. So the question reduces to: For odd n, is it possible to partition the triangular grid of side n into n lines, each line being a line of slope not 0,∞,-1, and each line containing exactly (n+1)/2 points? For n=3, we did it: the three lines we constructed (y=x, (1,3)-(2,1), (1,2)-(3,1)) each contain 2 points, and they partition the 6 points. So it worked. For n=5, we tried to find 5 disjoint sunny lines of size 3, but we only found 2 such lines, and they intersect. Could there be a different set of 5 sunny lines that cover all points? Possibly using lines that are not all of the same slope, but still each line of size 3. We need to see if there exists a partition of the 15 points into 5 lines of size 3. The only lines that contain 3 points in the 5x5 triangle are those that go through three lattice points. Let's list all such lines. The points are all (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤6. The possible lines that contain 3 points. We can think of the lattice points in the triangle. The lines of slope 1: y=x gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — that's 3. y=x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) but (3,4) not in triangle; so only 2. y=x-1 gives (2,1),(3,2) only 2. So only y=x gives 3 points. Lines of slope 2: y=2x-1 gives (1,1),(2,3) only; (3,5) not; so 2. y=2x gives (1,2),(2,4) only; (3,6) not; so 2. y=2x-3 gives (2,1),(3,3) only; so 2. y=2x-5? (3,1) only. So no 3. Slope 1/2: y=(1/2)x+1/2 gives (1,1),(3,2),(5,3) — that's 3. y=(1/2)x+1 gives (1,2),(3,3) only 2; (5,4) not; y=(1/2)x+? through (1,3) and (3,4) no; (2,1) and (4,2) gives (2,1),(3,1.5) no; so only that. Slope 1/3: y=(1/3)x+2/3 gives (1,1),(4,2) only 2; (7,3) no. So no. Slope -1: that's non-sunny. Slope -2: y=-2x+? For example, y=-2x+7 gives (1,5),(2,3) only 2; (3,1) gives -6+7=1, so (3,1) is also? Actually (3,1): -6+7=1, yes. So line through (1,5) and (3,1) has slope (1-5)/(3-1)=-4/2=-2, and equation y=-2x+7. Points: (1,5), (2,3) (since -4+7=3), (3,1) ( -6+7=1). So that's three points! Are they all in the triangle? (1,5): a+b=6, yes; (2,3): a+b=5, yes; (3,1): a+b=4, yes. So line y=-2x+7 contains three points: (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). That's slope -2, which is sunny (since not 0,∞,-1). So we have another line of size 3 with sunny slope. Also y=-2x+? Another: y=-2x+6 gives (1,4),(2,2),(3,0) no; (2,2) and (1,4) but (3,0) not. y=-2x+8 gives (1,6) not; (2,4) sum=6, (3,2) sum=5, (4,0) no. So only that. Similarly, slope -1/2: y=-1/2 x + c. For c=7/2? Actually we had (2,4) and (4,2) gives slope -1, not -1/2. Let's find lines of size 3 with slope -1/2. For instance, points (1,5) and (3,3) give slope (3-5)/(3-1) = -2/2=-1, not -1/2. (1,5) and (5,2) slope (2-5)/(5-1)= -3/4. (2,5) and (4,3) slope (3-5)/(4-2)= -2/2=-1. (1,4) and (3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1)= -2/2=-1. (1,4) and (5,1) slope (1-4)/(5-1)= -3/4. (2,4) and (4,2) slope -1. So no. Slope -3: (1,4) and (2,1) slope (1-4)/(2-1)=-3, points: (1,4),(2,1) only 2; (3,-2) no. So only the line with slope -2 we found, and the line with slope 1 and 1/2. So for n=5, the possible sunny lines of size 3 are: y=x (slope1), y=(1/2)x+1/2 (slope1/2), y=-2x+7 (slope -2). That's three lines. They are not disjoint? Check: y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3); y=(1/2)x+1/2 covers (1,1),(3,2),(5,3); y=-2x+7 covers (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). They share points: (1,1) in first two; (3,1) in third? Actually (3,1) is on third; (1,1) not on third. So they are not disjoint. Can we cover all 15 points with 5 sunny lines each of size 3? We would need 5 disjoint lines of size 3. But there are only 3 such lines in total, and they intersect. So it's impossible to have 5 disjoint lines of size 3. Could we have a partition where some lines have size 2 and some size 3? But then total sum of sizes would be less than 15 if we have 5 lines; but we need sum ≥15. If we use lines of size 3 and 2, the maximum sum is 3+3+3+3+2=14, which is <15, so cannot cover all points because each point is counted at least once; sum of sizes = total incidences, which must be at least 15. So with 5 lines, if any line has size ≤2, the total sum ≤ 3+3+3+3+2=14 <15. So all lines must have size exactly 3 and be disjoint. Since that's impossible, k=5 is impossible for n=5. So for odd n, k=n is possible only if we can partition the points into n lines each of size (n+1)/2. For n=3, we did it. For n=5, we couldn't. What about n=7? Possibly there is a pattern: For n=3, it works; for n=5, it doesn't. So maybe k=n is possible only when n=3? Or maybe there is a construction for odd n using lines of slope 1, 1/2, 1/3, etc., and their reflections? For n=7, the maximum sunny line size is floor(8/2)=4. So we need to partition the 28 points (n=7, N=7*8/2=28) into 7 lines each of size 4. Is that possible? We need to find 7 lines, each containing exactly 4 points from the triangle, and together covering all points. This is reminiscent of the fact that the triangular grid can be covered by lines of slope 1, 2, 3, ... but I'm not sure.

Let's step back. Perhaps the problem expects a characterization in terms of the integer part of n/2 or something like that. Let's compute more small n:

We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- For any n≥3, k=0 is possible by taking the n lines x+y=2,3,…,n+1.
- For n=3, k=1 and k=3 are possible; k=2 is impossible.
- For n=4, k=0,1,3 are possible; k=2 is impossible.
- For n=5, k=0,1,2,3 are possible.
- For n=6, k=0,1,3 are possible; k=2 is impossible.
- For n=7, k=0,1,2,3 are possible.
- For n=8, k=0,1,3 are possible; k=2 is impossible.
- For n=9, k=0,1,2,3 are possible? Unproven.
Thus the pattern is not simple.
